  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_7a7d35e0-178e-4062-b33c-0b6a600aa33b'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer16738939813846312452">Dear Reader,<br/><br/>If you have picked up this book with the hope of finding a simple and cheery tale, I'm afraid you have picked up the wrong book altogether.   <span id="freeText16738939813846312452" style="display:none">Dear Reader,<br /><br />If you have picked up this book with the hope of finding a simple and cheery tale, I'm afraid you have picked up the wrong book altogether. The story may seem cheery at first, when the Baudelaire children spend time in the company of some interesting reptiles and a giddy uncle, but don't be fooled. If you know anything at all about the unlucky Baudelaire children, you already know that even pleasant events lead down the same road to misery.<br /><br />In fact, within the pages you now hold in your hands, the three siblings endure a car accident, a terrible odor, a deadly serpent, a long knife, a large brass reading lamp, and the appearance of a person they'd hoped never to see again.<br /><br />I am bound to record these tragic events, but you are free to put this book back on the shelf and seek something lighter.<br /><br />With all due respect,<br /><br />Lemony Snicket</span>

<div id="review_2427133286" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2427133286" />
    <a title="Mischenko" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1194517-mischenko"><img alt="Mischenko" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587209348p2/1194517.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2427133286?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mischenko" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mischenko" href="/user/show/1194517-mischenko">Mischenko</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780064407670" href="/book/show/471928.The_Reptile_Room">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1194517-mischenko?shelf=middle-grade-children-s">middle-grade-children-s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2427133286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13599385731056331918">You can also see my review @ <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://readrantrockandroll.com/2018/07/03/the-reptile-room-a-series-of-unfortunate-events-2-by-lemony-snicket-book-review/'>https://readrantrockandroll.com/2018/...</a><br/><br/>In <i>The Bad Beginning</i>, the Baudelaire children lost their parents and everything they had before being shipped off to Count Olaf’s house. Count Olaf would be their caretaker until they were of age. Quickly the children realize that Count Olaf is evil and only wants to get his hands on their inherited fortune. After countless shenanigans, the children are finally whisked away from Count Olaf and Mr. Poe delivers them to Dr. Montg</span>
  <span id="freeText13599385731056331918" style="display:none">You can also see my review @ <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://readrantrockandroll.com/2018/07/03/the-reptile-room-a-series-of-unfortunate-events-2-by-lemony-snicket-book-review/">https://readrantrockandroll.com/2018/...</a><br><br>In <i>The Bad Beginning</i>, the Baudelaire children lost their parents and everything they had before being shipped off to Count Olaf’s house. Count Olaf would be their caretaker until they were of age. Quickly the children realize that Count Olaf is evil and only wants to get his hands on their inherited fortune. After countless shenanigans, the children are finally whisked away from Count Olaf and Mr. Poe delivers them to Dr. Montgomery a.k.a. Uncle Monty. This begins <i>The Reptile Room.</i><br><br><i>
  <b>Mr. Poe stepped up to the door and rang the doorbell that was one of the loudest the children have ever heard. After a moment’s pause they could hear approaching footsteps, and Violet, Klaus, and Sunny all looked at one another. They had no way of knowing, of course, that very soon there would be more misfortune within their unlucky family, but they nevertheless felt uneasy. Would Dr. Montgomery be a kind person? they wondered. Would he at least be better than Count Olaf? Could he possibly be worse?</b>
</i><br><br>Uncle Monty seems like a kind and safe person. The children like him and it seems they are finally safe to enjoy some coconut cream cake and an occasional evening movie. They each get their own room and their own chores for the trip they’ll be taking. Uncle Monty explains his plan to visit Peru with the children to go reptile exploring. Klaus is instructed to read books on reptiles, Sunny bites rope, and Violet works on traps that they need for the expedition. They’re very excited to go on the adventure, until they meet Uncle Monty’s assistant Stephano who is really Count Olaf in disguise.<br><br><a href="https://imgur.com/VRtXBem" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1530629111ra/25914873.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Everything spins out of control again as the children try to convince the others that Count Olaf is up to his old schemes again. Once again, it’s going to be up to the children to solve the problem.<br><br><i>
  <b>“Well, let’s try to hash this out,” Violet said, using an expression which here means “talk about something at length until we completely understand it.”</b>
</i><br><br>We really enjoyed this installment in the series, even more than the first book. I felt that there were positive aspects in this installment as well. For a time, the children feel safe and engage in normal everyday events, that is until Stephano shows up, but once again the strong children stand by each other and work to solve the problems they face.<br><br><a href="https://imgur.com/rzxYEqU" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1530629111ra/25914872.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>The writing is great and we thought it was really easy to follow just like the first book. We loved the illustrations and all the new vocabulary words/phrases that are introduced for children to learn. It was a nice surprise that multiple times throughout the book there were events that were not in the movie which kept us interested.<br><br>Overall, this is another winner for us and left completely open for the third book <i>The Wide Window. </i><br><br>5*****</span>
  <a data-text-id="13599385731056331918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11771399230254819504"><b>As the good little fan of Lemony Snicket I am</b>, I've decided to reread THEM ALL because I'm watching the Netflix show. And by "good little fan" here I mean "a rabidly obsessed grape who is flailing unnaturally enthusiastically at all hours because loves this series so much."<br/><br/>So this is a reread! I was about 13 when I was first reading this series! And 10 years later and I still adore it to pieces!<br/><br/>Although I admit this one isn't as good as the first one, just because Violet's invention was a really</span>
  <span id="freeText11771399230254819504" style="display:none"><b>As the good little fan of Lemony Snicket I am</b>, I've decided to reread THEM ALL because I'm watching the Netflix show. And by "good little fan" here I mean "a rabidly obsessed grape who is flailing unnaturally enthusiastically at all hours because loves this series so much."<br /><br />So this is a reread! I was about 13 when I was first reading this series! And 10 years later and I still adore it to pieces!<br /><br />Although I admit this one isn't as good as the first one, just because Violet's invention was a really small lockpick instead of something as cool as the grappling hook in the last one (lockpicks are still cool, dude, but I'm just sayin' it wasn't as unique) and <b>the actual mystery of convincing everyone that Olaf was dressed up as Stefano was pretty brief.</b><br /><br />But you know why this book is THE TRUEST FORM OF PAIN??? it starts of so lovely! The Baudelaires FINALLY get a nice uncle who makes them coconut cake (#winning) and encourages them in their interests and loves them and -- THEN IT ALL GETS BRUTALLY SNATCHED AWAY.<br /><br />THANKS SNICKET.<br /><br />But I guess we were warned.<br /><br />(I freaking love this series omg.)<br /><br />(And FYI the show is amazing so far.)</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer938056621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11285117268220516502">So, Celyn and I cruised on to book 2, pausing only for breath.<br/><br/>This book sets a pattern, a formula, that is repeated in each of the books we've read so far.<br/><br/>The children move to a new place. <br/><br/>All the adults are idiots. <br/><br/>Count Olaaf arrives in disguise and at some point a selected member of his theatre troop will show up, also in disguise (someone may have been bumped off to make space for one or both of them). <br/><br/>The children see through his disguise in an instant.<br/><br/>The adults don't believe them.<br/><br/>Count Ol</span>
  <span id="freeText11285117268220516502" style="display:none">So, Celyn and I cruised on to book 2, pausing only for breath.<br /><br />This book sets a pattern, a formula, that is repeated in each of the books we've read so far.<br /><br />The children move to a new place. <br /><br />All the adults are idiots. <br /><br />Count Olaaf arrives in disguise and at some point a selected member of his theatre troop will show up, also in disguise (someone may have been bumped off to make space for one or both of them). <br /><br />The children see through his disguise in an instant.<br /><br />The adults don't believe them.<br /><br />Count Olaaf insinuates his way into their lives and gets them to refer to him by his assumed name (via some kind of threat).<br /><br />Interesting words are introduced and defined amusingly in context by the narrator.<br /><br />Sunny makes short random utterances that are interpreted by the narrator as lengthy and cogent observations.<br /><br />The three children use their given skills (respectively inventor, bookworm, biter) to solve the problem of not letting Count Olaaf gain control of them.<br /><br />At the end Mr Poe is called in and Olaaf's disguise is revealed.<br /><br />Count Olaaf runs away while Mr Poe coughs then phones the police.<br /><br />Rinse and repeat.<br /><br />Because this is book 2, and because Lemony Snicket is an entertaining writer, the formula has not yet grown old.<br /><br />Things get darker in this book. Actual murder is done. Olaaf repeatedly threatens the children with a knife and with death.<br /><br />As the books progress there is a slow progression toward the cartoony and surreal. In this book though the action is still largely believable and the world recognizably 'real' - only the bizarre reptiles referred to in the reptile room take us toward make-believe, and only Sunny's contribution in 'biting rope into shorter lengths' takes us toward cartoony.<br /><br />This is then, a well written and entertaining book that establishes a formula LS proceeds to beat to death every book hereafter (at least as far as we've read). Fortunately LS's skills mean that this isn't as painful experience as one might think.<br /><br /><br /> <a href="http://eepurl.com/cimnK1" rel="nofollow noopener">Join my 3-emails-a-year newsletter #prizes</a><br /><br /><br />….</span>
  <a data-text-id="11285117268220516502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3391456083" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3391456083" />
    <a title="Brooke Nelson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25910969-brooke-nelson"><img alt="Brooke Nelson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603074616p2/25910969.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3391456083?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brooke Nelson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brooke Nelson" href="/user/show/25910969-brooke-nelson">Brooke Nelson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25910969-brooke-nelson?shelf=superior-writing">superior-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25910969-brooke-nelson?shelf=god-tier-protagonists">god-tier-protagonists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25910969-brooke-nelson?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25910969-brooke-nelson?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25910969-brooke-nelson?shelf=holy-moly-i-am-in-love">holy-moly-i-am-in-love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3391456083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9575077617803343892"><b>
  <i>Violet Baudelaire is a revolution in the female character construct.</i>
</b><br/><br/>This book is so funny and imaginative and delightfully strange.<br/><br/>But let's talk about Violet. What a positively outstanding character. Gentle and kind and funny and intelligent and sensible and feminine <i>and</i> brave. It just doesn't get better than that.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3391456786' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Book 1: <i>The Bad Beginning</i> Review</a><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3391456154' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Book 3: <i>The Wide Window</i> Review</a><br/><br/><a href='https://linktr.ee/brookenelsonauthor' rel='nofollow noopener'>(Connect with me.)</a></span>
  <span id="freeText9575077617803343892" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>Violet Baudelaire is a revolution in the female character construct.</i>
</b><br /><br />This book is so funny and imaginative and delightfully strange.<br /><br />But let's talk about Violet. What a positively outstanding character. Gentle and kind and funny and intelligent and sensible and feminine <i>and</i> brave. It just doesn't get better than that.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3391456786" rel="nofollow noopener">My Book 1: <i>The Bad Beginning</i> Review</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3391456154" rel="nofollow noopener">My Book 3: <i>The Wide Window</i> Review</a><br /><br /><a href="https://linktr.ee/brookenelsonauthor" rel="nofollow noopener">(Connect with me.)</a></span>

<div id="review_2240903713" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2240903713" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2240903713?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781405208680" href="/book/show/196408.The_Reptile_Room">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2240903713" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15602532359646125609">Wow, this series tanked hard - and so damn soon after a great first book! I kinda expected it to dip in quality gradually, like every series does at some point, but so quickly and by so much in just the second book? That’s impressive! <br/><br/>So the orphaned Baudelaire children, Violet, Klaus and Sunny, are sent to a new distant relative to stay with. This time it’s their Uncle Monty, who’s a herpetologist (a scientist who studies amphibians/reptiles). Then Count Olaf appears and the same charade from t</span>
  <span id="freeText15602532359646125609" style="display:none">Wow, this series tanked hard - and so damn soon after a great first book! I kinda expected it to dip in quality gradually, like every series does at some point, but so quickly and by so much in just the second book? That’s impressive! <br /><br />So the orphaned Baudelaire children, Violet, Klaus and Sunny, are sent to a new distant relative to stay with. This time it’s their Uncle Monty, who’s a herpetologist (a scientist who studies amphibians/reptiles). Then Count Olaf appears and the same charade from the first book is repeated and it’s over. Really? Really. <br /><br />I guess good for the kids that they wound up, however temporarily, with a nice relative after the misery of staying with Count Olaf but unfortunately pleasant things aren’t in the least bit interesting to read (that’s why I can’t abide Alexander McCall Smith’s books or that abominable Lake Wobegon crap)! And once Olaf does appear, in disguise, we instantly know it’s him so we have to wait for the adult characters - whose contrived stupidity has already gotten annoying - to figure it out. And boy does that swiftly get tedious when nothing else is happening! <br /><br />I liked that the series still has an edge to it (by children’s books standards) with the fate of Uncle Monty, the book is well-written and Count Olaf remains an interesting character. But for the most part The Reptile Room was an immensely boring and worthless addition to the series. Thank jeebus these books are quick to read otherwise I’d’ve packed it in long before the end! Hopefully the next book picks up by cutting out the filler and actually having a semi-compelling story! </span>
  <a data-text-id="15602532359646125609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1269224331435754785"><i>“It is like walking up the stairs to your bedroom in the dark, and thinking there is one more stair than there is. The Baudelaire orphans were crying not only for their Uncle Monty, but for their own parents, and this dark and curious feeling of falling that accompanies every great loss.”</i><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1563286414ra/27836627.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>This second volume was just as enjoyable as the first... and oh how muc</span>

  <span id="freeText7883212354952363680" style="display:none">It's a cool, cloudy gray grim day...<br>It's 25th January.. well..it reminds me with the <b>
  <i>event</i>

</i>, let's back to the Baudelaire's Series of Unfortunate Events... told by the dark fun, Lemony Snicket.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485204698i/21793256._SX540_.jpg" width="449" height="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><br></span>

How is Mr. Poe so entirely incompetent? <div id="review_1881334046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1881334046" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7477266-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433820623p2/7477266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1881334046?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/7477266-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780061757143" href="/book/show/10135768-the-reptile-room">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7477266-sarah?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7477266-sarah?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7477266-sarah?shelf=z2017">z2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1881334046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8142572524573647844">3.5ish Stars. <br/><br/>I was actually liking this one a lot more than the first one right up until the unfortunate event. The unfortunate event was indeed unfortunate but that's not what made me stop liking it as much. I felt it got kind of boring and it dragged out. I did still love the writing and the majority of the storytelling though. <br/><br/>What I found interesting though is how this Lemony Snicket storyteller keeps explaining words to his readers when in the story the adults do it but the children always</span>
  <span id="freeText8142572524573647844" style="display:none">3.5ish Stars. <br /><br />I was actually liking this one a lot more than the first one right up until the unfortunate event. The unfortunate event was indeed unfortunate but that's not what made me stop liking it as much. I felt it got kind of boring and it dragged out. I did still love the writing and the majority of the storytelling though. <br /><br />What I found interesting though is how this Lemony Snicket storyteller keeps explaining words to his readers when in the story the adults do it but the children always know what it means and it shows how the adults belittle the orphans. So either Lemony Snicket is also belittling his young readers, he's doing it to be ironic or he's doing it because it's actually quite helpful. If I had to guess, I would say he's being ironic. Lemony Snicket paints a bad picture of adults in these books and even though this Lemony Snicket person is telling the story with compassion and sympathy, at the end of the day he is an adult and adults are incompetent people who don't listen to children and belittle children. I thought it was quite sweet for Snicket to explain what a word means. Sometimes he uses colourful language to describe something and he's just telling his readers what it means. He's not only teaching his readers about the definition of words either. He explains what dramatic irony is in this book and I just think that's great. I feel like both books I have read so far have also had life lessons in them. The first one was all about how bad things can happen and it can seem quite unfair but you have to keep on going. If the children had just accepted what Count Olaf was doing, they would be in a very bad position. They overcame Olaf's mean tricks because they didn't lose hope and they kept fighting. The lesson in this book was more to do with grieving and regrets about life I think. There was a lot on loss and life after the death of a loved one and also a bit about regrets and what you only wish you could have done. <br /><br />... of course maybe this is just a story that is nothing but entertaining because it is a children's book and I do have a tendency to over-analyse absolutely everything!!! Anyways I will stop my review now. I would recommend this and I would read more by Lemony Snicket. <br /><br />~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~<br /><br />“For Beatrice—<br />My love for you shall live forever.<br />You, however, did not.”<br /><br />“I wish—and I'm sure you wish as well—that we could go back in time and warn him, but we can't, and that is that.”<br /><br />“I think we'll always miss our parents. But I think we can miss them without being miserable all the time. After all, they wouldn't want us to be miserable.”<br /><br />“One of the most difficult things to think about in life is one's regrets. Something will happen to you, and you will do the wrong thing, and for years afterward you will wish you had done something different.”<br /><br />“It is a curious thing, the death of a loved one. We all know that our time in this world is limited, and that eventually all of us will end up underneath some sheet, never to wake up. And yet it is always a surprise when it happens to someone we know.”<br /><br />“It is remarkable that different people will have different thoughts when they look at the same thing.”<br /><br />“How did you do that?” Mr. Poe asked. “Nice girls shouldn't know how to do such things.”<br />“My sister is a nice girl,” Klaus said, “and she knows how to do all sorts of things.”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8142572524573647844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_783362052" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/783362052" />
    <a title="Carmen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25353295-carmen"><img alt="Carmen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412617445p2/25353295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/783362052?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carmen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carmen" href="/user/show/25353295-carmen">Carmen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone and Everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=he-says">he-says</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=traditionally-published">traditionally-published</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer783362052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6399497986626968556">These books are amazing. This book is a little darker than the first. It tells you right from the beginning that Uncle Monty will be killed. Other examples of darkness: <br/><br/>p. 51 <i>...he would slit the throats of the Baudelaire orphans as easily as you or I might eat a small butter cookie. </i><br/><br/>p.76 <i>If I wanted to harm you, orphan, your blood would already be pouring down these stairs like a waterfall.</i><br/><br/>p.88 <i>The hallway was strangely quiet, and blank as the eyes of a skull.</i><br/><br/>p.96 <i>It is a curious thing, the dea</i></span>
  <span id="freeText6399497986626968556" style="display:none">These books are amazing. This book is a little darker than the first. It tells you right from the beginning that Uncle Monty will be killed. Other examples of darkness: <br /><br />p. 51 <i>...he would slit the throats of the Baudelaire orphans as easily as you or I might eat a small butter cookie. </i><br /><br />p.76 <i>If I wanted to harm you, orphan, your blood would already be pouring down these stairs like a waterfall.</i><br /><br />p.88 <i>The hallway was strangely quiet, and blank as the eyes of a skull.</i><br /><br />p.96 <i>It is a curious thing, the death of a loved one. We all know that our time in this world is limited, and that eventually all of us will end up underneath some sheet, never to wake up. And yet it is always a surprise when it happens to someone we know. It is like walking up the stairs to your bedroom in the dark, and thinking there is one more stair than there is. Your foot falls down, through the air, and there is a sickly moment of dark surprise as you try to readjust the way you thought of things.</i><br /><br />This last thought, about losing a person you love, is so true and poignant. I love these books for this reason. <br /><br />One of the most brilliant and touching aspects of these novels is the support, love, and intelligence of the Baudelaire orphans. Snicket really emphasizes the great blessing it can be to have brothers and sisters. One particular thing that I noticed in this book was how helpful it is to have people with shared memories. One of the greatest things about siblings is you have shared memories about how your parents were, how your house was, what growing up was like, etc. This is mentioned in The Reptile Room (page 39).<br /><br />There are a few instances of profanity in this book. No profanity was used in A Bad Beginning. "Damn" and "hell" are the words used, and they are used by the villain and frowned upon by the orphans. I don't take any issue with the profanity in this book - ten-year-olds will have heard these already and I thought they were appropriate in the context (pages 94 and 98).<br /><br />The most hilarious scene is when Mr. Poe panics in the Reptile Room of Uncle Monty's house. I laughed out loud.<br /><br />Here is a great example of Snicket's writing style:<br /><br /><i>It is very difficult, experts have told us, to find a needle in a haystack, which is why “needle in a haystack” has become a rather hackneyed phrase meaning “something that is difficult to find.” The reason it is difficult to find a needle in a haystack, of course, is that out of all the things in a haystack, the needle is only one of them. If, however, you were looking for anything in a haystack, that wouldn't be difficult at all, because once you started sifting through the haystack you would most certainly find something: hay, of course, but also dirt, bugs, a few farming tools, and maybe even a man who had escaped from prison and was hiding there.</i> p.161 <br /><br />I also adore the feminist spirit shown in this book. Upon learning that Violet Baudelaire has picked a lock, Mr. Poe reacts with dismay:<br /><br /><i>“How did you do that?” Mr. Poe asked. “Nice girls shouldn't know how to do such things.”<br />“My sister is a nice girl,” Klaus said, “and she knows how to do all sorts of things.” </i>p. 168 <br /><br />Way to go, Klaus! He stands up for his sister and also understands that she can be "nice" and also be a "mechanical genius" at the same time. A very powerful message for girls.<br /><br />It's obvious throughout the book that the siblings have each others backs. They are all intelligent in their own way, they all contribute to their plans. They have such a strong bond that is only getting stronger the more it is tested by Olaf and other villains. <br /><br />A highlight of this book is that Sunny finally starts using real words instead of her normal gibberish. I think she has two real words in here: "Aha!" and "Brilliant." She is becoming more independent and more communicative and it is a joy to see. Just as in real life, when you watch children grow up and become their own people with their own opinions and distinct personalities, so Sunny is illustrated here with her burgeoning vocabulary and personality. <br /><br />Once again I want to mention how smart and educational these books are in addition to being funny and entrancing. Here are some words and concepts children will learn when reading this book: dramatic irony, alcove, segue, giddy, nemesis, surveillance, vainglorious, preempt, generic, brummagem, adding insult to injury, unblemished, hackneyed, inner sanctum, strangulatory, conjunction, tenebrous, hue, perpetuate his deception, swarthy, ruffian, and lampoon. Most of these are explained in a fun and stylistic way by Mr. Snicket, but others will have the child reaching for the dictionary.<br /><br />I would also like to note the subtle Agatha Christie joke on page 126. Adults who are fans of Christie's work will get a chuckle out of this.<br /><br />Kudos to Lemony Snicket for another thoroughly charming read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6399497986626968556" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="B Schrodinger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger"><img alt="B Schrodinger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572325701p2/5448792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1447883591?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B Schrodinger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B Schrodinger" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger">B Schrodinger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1447883591" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5710925851121084723">In this second volume, the Baudelaire children are sent to stay with another distant relative who is a herpetologist - studies snakes, therefore the reptile room of the title. What proceeds is another adventure of mishaps and contrived situations that see the children triumph, yet not at the same time. <br/><br/>There is nothing new to report here, I felt the same way about this novel as the last - a little bit creeped out, a lot bored. There is the explaining of words, which I do like and the hint of som</span>
  <span id="freeText5710925851121084723" style="display:none">In this second volume, the Baudelaire children are sent to stay with another distant relative who is a herpetologist - studies snakes, therefore the reptile room of the title. What proceeds is another adventure of mishaps and contrived situations that see the children triumph, yet not at the same time. <br /><br />There is nothing new to report here, I felt the same way about this novel as the last - a little bit creeped out, a lot bored. There is the explaining of words, which I do like and the hint of something larger and deeper when the author refers to himself, but the majority is humdrum classic villainous villainry. While the motives of the villain are known, it is never really explained why some nations fitted his plan. But, as usual I think I am thinking too much about it.<br /><br />My partner's response to my felling of naffs about this was a telling off. "It's a children's book so read it like one!" I don't know if I can anymore. I still enjoy some children's books, and they are probably books that I would have loved as a kid also. But these books, I don't know if I would have liked them as a kid. <br /><br />Anyway, I have been told they get much better, especially from the next book. For my own sake I sure hope so. If I do end up reading it and it is just like the last two I am going to feel like I have wasted my time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5710925851121084723" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Violet once again shows her cunning knowledge in tools and devices, Klaus's bookish personality saved their lives, and the adorable Sunny is still that too-mature-to-be-an-infant baby. Hope these kids find their perfect guardians soon and get out of all of their misfortunes. Off to the third book, which is "The Wide Window"!</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50620706-alaina?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer6073841391720957281">Count Olaf is back at it again!<br/><br/>Violet, Klaus, and Sunny are here too. They are getting closer and closer to a great future after learning about their parents death. They have a new guardian in their lives, Monty. However, he still seems naive just like every other adult in this series. Then there's Count Olaf who is still trying to take the kids fortune. <br/><br/>Sunny is still my absolute favorite character. She just always makes me laugh no matter what. The kid's got spunk and I will forever love to wa</span>
  <span id="freeText6073841391720957281" style="display:none">Count Olaf is back at it again!<br /><br />Violet, Klaus, and Sunny are here too. They are getting closer and closer to a great future after learning about their parents death. They have a new guardian in their lives, Monty. However, he still seems naive just like every other adult in this series. Then there's Count Olaf who is still trying to take the kids fortune. <br /><br />Sunny is still my absolute favorite character. She just always makes me laugh no matter what. The kid's got spunk and I will forever love to watch her character grow. Then there's her older siblings, Violet and Klaus who are equally likable and hilarious. Yet, Sunny will forever be my favorite. <br /><br />Even though I love the kids because of how smart, sassy, and hilarious they are. It does frustrate me to no end how the "adults" in these books never seem to believe them. Plus, they are so naive too. The adults definitely do frustrate me - except for Count Olaf, because the things he does just crack me up. I can't take him seriously, ever.<br /><br />Overall, I really enjoyed this book. It's dark humor gives me life. I can't wait to dive into the next book.</span>

<div id="review_67661656" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/67661656" />
    <a title="Lesley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2634051-lesley"><img alt="Lesley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578642440p2/2634051.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/67661656?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lesley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lesley" href="/user/show/2634051-lesley">Lesley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2634051-lesley?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer67661656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13275325969150815958">For anyone who isn't familiar with Lemony Snicket's A Series of Unfortunate Events, it's a 13 part children's book series that follows the lives of the three Baudelaire children, Violet, Klaus and Sunny after their parents perish in a fire that destroyed their family home. Cheerful right? Honestly, it's wonderful.<br/><br/>The story is told from the narrative perspective of Lemony Snicket. The concept, the literary devices, the language used are all wholly unique to me. The fact that Daniel Handler create</span>
  <span id="freeText13275325969150815958" style="display:none">For anyone who isn't familiar with Lemony Snicket's A Series of Unfortunate Events, it's a 13 part children's book series that follows the lives of the three Baudelaire children, Violet, Klaus and Sunny after their parents perish in a fire that destroyed their family home. Cheerful right? Honestly, it's wonderful.<br /><br />The story is told from the narrative perspective of Lemony Snicket. The concept, the literary devices, the language used are all wholly unique to me. The fact that Daniel Handler created his pseudonym, Lemony Snicket to actually be part of the story is just pure genius in my eyes. Especially since the effect was so positively received. Snicket speaks to the reader directly, explaining things as he goes along and simply being thoroughly entertaining in the process. One thing I really love is that he never speaks down to the reader who is obviously meant to be a child. Even when blatantly explaining things it's always done in a round about fashion to the effect of you're terribly clever so I'm sure you already know this but I suppose I'll say it just for the sake of things. Absolutely brilliant. Anyway. Moving on.<br /><br /><br />The Reptile Room is the second novella in the series. The first volume, The Bad Beginning, does a fabulous job of setting up our main protagonists and antagonists. We are with the Baudelaire's when they hear the news of their parents death. We are with them as they get sent to live with a distant relative, Count Olaf, who turns out to be the antithesis of all evil and wants nothing more then to get rid of the children so he can inherit their large fortune which won't be available til Violet's 18th birthday (who is only 12 at the start of out story). We are there as they use their wits and courage to escape from Count Olaf's grasp and we are there as the hold on to hope that their lives can at least be a fraction of what they were meant to be.<br /><br />The beginning of The Reptile Room sees the children sent off to live with another relative. This one infinity more satisfactory then Count Olaf. Uncle Monty is a kind and loving herpetologist (studier of snakes and reptiles) who instantly takes the children into his care with love and devotion only to have Count Olaf show up again but this time disguised as Monty's new assistant, Stephano. The children instantly see through this facade but aren't able to make Uncle Monty understand in time to prevent his death.<br /><br /><br /><br />The Reptile Room is a brilliant follow up to The Bad Beginning. With each volume we get a better insight into these three fine children and what it is that makes them so remarkable. Handlers depiction of the children is perfect from the start with simple but very effective descriptions of the children and their unique talents and personalities being instigated from the first chapter of the first book. Violet we learn is an inventor and has a mind for the inner workings of machines. We also learn that when she is busy thinking she ties her hair up with a ribbon so she can focus on her thoughts. Klaus is an avid reader, constantly researching subjects of interest and we learn that young Sunny is much unlike regular babies in the fact that she likes to use her few teeth to bite extremely hard objects.<br /><br /><br />All three children use their respective skills and interests to their advantage time and again throughout the series.<br /><br />One thing that I really love about this series is how it has been marketed as a complete package. The writing and the stories alone are incredible but on top of that we have the invention of Lemony Snicket as this mysterious author figure and then we have the design of the actual books themselves. The books were originally published in hard cover. The pages are cut somewhat jagged and haphazard, creating a wonderfully distressed effect but one of the most important and beautiful aspects is the incredible illustrations by Brett Helquist. The cover art, including the inside cover and an illustration that appears at the beginning of each chapter is all designed by Helquist's skillful hand. The effect they have is almost difficult to encapsulate in words. The illustrations capture the tone and sensibilities of the books perfectly and actually assist to engage the reader further into Snicket's complex world of murder and mystery. One of if not, the most perfect children's series I've ever had the great fortune to stumble upon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13275325969150815958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1888315834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8668574531377798116">I distinctly remember this book being my favourite out of the whole series as a child and after rereading it, that has definitely proven to be true! But I couldn't really tell you why it's my favourite, it just grabs me like no other in the series did! I just love the Baudelaires and how each of them are so unique yet complement each other so well in escaping Olaf time after time. I'm truly enjoying my reread of this series so much and with two books down and eleven to go, I'm already dreading m</span>
  <span id="freeText8668574531377798116" style="display:none">I distinctly remember this book being my favourite out of the whole series as a child and after rereading it, that has definitely proven to be true! But I couldn't really tell you why it's my favourite, it just grabs me like no other in the series did! I just love the Baudelaires and how each of them are so unique yet complement each other so well in escaping Olaf time after time. I'm truly enjoying my reread of this series so much and with two books down and eleven to go, I'm already dreading my reread being over!</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xBWCQEqDOJ+g0qFpY2zUhSXyzdNUhO7hxMdA7YOQRGVe1pZyJVZqW/tEiMigcJZi6WbfAOw7Wf4SeIOHQjOUvw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('XtnwAz2jPi72BGS96WVQwronfpRcrEmZGjLBLAnQp1nEGuQxUnZs6q2STRwqeRIldrNsR+QT/obMjQJGyHN3gw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>
